<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Conservatism</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Political philosophy focused on retaining traditional social institutions</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Conservatives" redirects here. For specific political parties, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(disambiguation)" title="Conservative Party (disambiguation)">Conservative Party</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about conservatism as a political and social philosophy. For other uses of conservatism and conservative, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Conservatism_(disambiguation)" title="Conservatism (disambiguation)">Conservatism (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Conservation_movement" title="Conservation movement">Conservation movement</a>.</div>

<p><b>Conservatism</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_culture" title="Philosophy of culture">cultural</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_philosophy" title="Social philosophy">social</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">political philosophy</a> that seeks to promote and to preserve traditional social <a href="/wiki/Institution" title="Institution">institutions</a> and practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-2">[2]</a></sup> The central tenets of conservatism may vary in relation to the <i>status quo</i> of the <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> in which it appears. In <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a>, conservatives seek to preserve a range of institutions such as <a href="/wiki/Organized_religion" title="Organized religion">organized religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">parliamentary government</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Right_to_property" title="Right to property">property rights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeywood201269_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeywood201269-3">[3]</a></sup> Conservatives tend to favor institutions and practices that guarantee stability and evolved gradually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-2">[2]</a></sup> Adherents of conservatism often oppose modernism and seek a return to traditional values, though different groups of conservatives may choose different traditional values to preserve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first established use of the term in a political context originated in 1818 with <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois-Ren%C3%A9_de_Chateaubriand" title="François-René de Chateaubriand">François-René de Chateaubriand</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> during the period of <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_Restoration_in_France" title="Bourbon Restoration in France">Bourbon Restoration</a> that sought to roll back the policies of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>. Historically associated with <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_politics" title="Right-wing politics">right-wing politics</a>, the term has since been used to describe <a href="#Forms">a wide range of views</a>. There is no single set of policies regarded as conservative because the meaning of conservatism depends on what is considered traditional in a given place and time. Conservative thought has varied considerably as it has adapted itself to existing traditions and national cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeywood201266_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeywood201266-6">[6]</a></sup> For example, some conservatives advocate for greater <a href="/wiki/Economic_interventionism" title="Economic interventionism">economic intervention</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVincent200979_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVincent200979-7">[7]</a></sup> while others advocate for a more <i><a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez faire</a></i> free-market economic system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVincent200978_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVincent200978-8">[8]</a></sup> Thus, conservatives from different parts of the world—each upholding their respective traditions—may disagree on a wide range of issues. <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">Edmund Burke</a>, an 18th-century politician who opposed the French Revolution but supported the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>, is credited as one of the main theorists of conservatism in the 1790s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-4"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Themes">Themes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Themes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some political scientists such as <a href="/wiki/Samuel_P._Huntington" title="Samuel P. Huntington">Samuel P. Huntington</a>, see conservatism as situational. Under this definition, conservatives are seen as defending the established institutions of their time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Quintin_Hogg,_Baron_Hailsham_of_St_Marylebone" title="Quintin Hogg, Baron Hailsham of St Marylebone">Quintin Hogg</a>, the chairman of the British <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> in 1959: "Conservatism is not so much a philosophy as an attitude, a constant force, performing a timeless function in the development of a free society, and corresponding to a deep and permanent requirement of human nature itself".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Conservatism is often used as a generic term to describe a "right-wing viewpoint occupying the political spectrum between <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascism</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-1">[1]</a></sup> Despite the lack of a universal definition, certain themes can be recognised as common across conservative thought.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tradition">Tradition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Tradition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Michael_Oakeshott" title="Michael Oakeshott">Michael Oakeshott</a>, "To be conservative ... is to prefer the familiar to the unknown, to prefer the tried to the untried, fact to mystery, the actual to the possible, the limited to the unbounded, the near to the distant, the sufficient to the superabundant, the convenient to the perfect, present laughter to utopian bliss."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Such traditionalism may be a reflection of trust in time-tested methods of social organisation, giving 'votes to the dead'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeywood201766_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeywood201766-13">[13]</a></sup> Traditions may also be steeped in a sense of identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeywood201766_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeywood201766-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hierarchy">Hierarchy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Hierarchy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In contrast to the tradition-based definition of conservatism, some political theorists such as <a href="/wiki/Corey_Robin" title="Corey Robin">Corey Robin</a> define conservatism primarily in terms of a general defense of <a href="/wiki/Social_inequality" title="Social inequality">social</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">economic inequality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> In that way right-wing politics supports the view that certain social orders and hierarchies are inevitable, natural, normal, or desirable, typically supporting this position on the basis of <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a>, economics, or tradition.  From this perspective, conservatism is less an attempt to uphold old institutions and more "a meditation on—and theoretical rendition of—the felt experience of having power, seeing it threatened, and trying to win it back".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Conversely, some conservatives may argue that they are seeking less to protect their own power than they are seeking to protect "inalienable rights" and promote norms and rules that they believe should stand timeless and eternal, applying to each citizen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Realism">Realism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Realism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Conservatism has been called a "philosophy of human imperfection" by <a href="/wiki/No%C3%ABl_O%27Sullivan" title="Noël O'Sullivan">Noël O'Sullivan</a>, reflecting among its adherents a negative view of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a> and pessimism of the potential to improve it through 'utopian' schemes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeywood201767_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeywood201767-17">[17]</a></sup> The "intellectual godfather of the realist right", <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>, argued that the <a href="/wiki/State_of_nature" title="State of nature">state of nature</a> for humans was "poor, nasty, brutish, and short", requiring centralised authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms">Forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberal_conservatism">Liberal conservatism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Liberal conservatism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liberal_conservatism" title="Liberal conservatism">Liberal conservatism</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Conservative_liberalism" title="Conservative liberalism">Conservative liberalism</a>.</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Liberal_conservatism" title="Liberal conservatism">Liberal conservatism</a> incorporates the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_liberal" title="Classical liberal">classical liberal</a> view of minimal government intervention in the economy. Individuals should be free to participate in the market and generate wealth without government interference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcAnulla200671_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcAnulla200671-20">[20]</a></sup> However, individuals cannot be thoroughly depended on to act responsibly in other spheres of life, therefore liberal conservatives believe that a strong state is necessary to ensure law and order and social institutions are needed to nurture a sense of duty and responsibility to the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcAnulla200671_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcAnulla200671-20">[20]</a></sup> Liberal conservatism is a variant of conservatism that is strongly influenced by <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a> stances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>As these latter two terms have had different meanings over time and across countries, liberal conservatism also has a wide variety of meanings. Historically, the term often referred to the combination of <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economic liberalism</a>, which champions <i><a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a></i> markets, with the classical conservatism concern for established <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">tradition</a>, respect for authority and religious values. It contrasted itself with <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">classical liberalism</a>, which supported <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_for_the_individual" title="Freedom for the individual">freedom for the individual</a> in both the economic and social spheres.
</p><p>Over time, the general conservative ideology in many countries adopted fiscally conservative arguments and the term liberal conservatism was replaced with conservatism. This is also the case in countries where liberal economic ideas have been the tradition such as the United States and are thus considered conservative. In other countries where liberal conservative movements have entered the political mainstream, such as <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, the terms liberal and conservative may be synonymous. The liberal conservative tradition in the United States combines the economic <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individualism</a> of the classical liberals with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burkean" title="Burkean">Burkean</a> form of conservatism (which has also become part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_conservative" title="American conservative">American conservative</a> tradition, such as in the writings of <a href="/wiki/Russell_Kirk" title="Russell Kirk">Russell Kirk</a>).
</p><p>A secondary meaning for the term liberal conservatism that has developed in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> is a combination of more modern conservative (less traditionalist) views with those of <a href="/wiki/Social_liberalism" title="Social liberalism">social liberalism</a>. This has developed as an opposition to the more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collectivism_and_individualism" title="Collectivism and individualism">collectivist</a> views of <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a>. Often this involves stressing conservative views of <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free market</a> economics and belief in individual responsibility, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communitarian" title="Communitarian">communitarian</a> views on defence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_rights" title="Civil rights">civil rights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmentalism</a> and support for a limited <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a>. In continental Europe, this is sometimes also translated into English as social conservatism.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Libertarian_conservatism">Libertarian conservatism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Libertarian conservatism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_conservatism" title="Libertarian conservatism">Libertarian conservatism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Libertarian_conservatism" title="Libertarian conservatism">Libertarian conservatism</a> describes certain political ideologies most prominently within the United States which combine <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism_in_the_United_States" title="Libertarianism in the United States">libertarian</a> economic issues with aspects of conservatism. Its four main branches are <a href="/wiki/Constitutionalism" title="Constitutionalism">constitutionalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paleolibertarianism" title="Paleolibertarianism">paleolibertarianism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_conservatism" title="American conservatism">small government conservatism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christian_libertarianism" title="Christian libertarianism">Christian libertarianism</a>. They generally differ from <a href="/wiki/Paleoconservatism" title="Paleoconservatism">paleoconservatives</a>, in that they favor more <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">personal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economic_freedom" title="Economic freedom">economic freedom</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agorist" title="Agorist">Agorists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Edward_Konkin_III" title="Samuel Edward Konkin III">Samuel Edward Konkin III</a> labeled libertarian conservatism <a href="/wiki/Right-libertarianism" title="Right-libertarianism">right-libertarianism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nlm_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-nlm-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-int_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-int-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>In contrast to paleoconservatives, libertarian conservatives support strict <i>laissez-faire</i> policies such as <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a>, opposition to any national bank and opposition to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regulations" title="Regulations">business regulations</a>. They are vehemently opposed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_regulation" title="Environmental regulation">environmental regulations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corporate_welfare" title="Corporate welfare">corporate welfare</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsidies" title="Subsidies">subsidies</a> and other areas of economic intervention.
</p><p>Many conservatives, especially in the United States, believe that the government should not play a major role in regulating business and managing the economy. They typically oppose efforts to charge high tax rates and to redistribute income to assist the poor. Such efforts, they argue, only serve to exacerbate the scourge of unemployment and poverty by lessening the ability for businesses to hire employees due to higher tax impositions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiscal_conservatism">Fiscal conservatism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fiscal conservatism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_conservatism" title="Fiscal conservatism">Fiscal conservatism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TeaPartyByFreedomFan.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/TeaPartyByFreedomFan.JPG/220px-TeaPartyByFreedomFan.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/TeaPartyByFreedomFan.JPG/330px-TeaPartyByFreedomFan.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/TeaPartyByFreedomFan.JPG/440px-TeaPartyByFreedomFan.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TeaPartyByFreedomFan.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>2009 <a href="/wiki/Taxpayer_March_on_Washington" title="Taxpayer March on Washington">Taxpayer March on Washington</a> as conservative protesters walk down <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Avenue_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Pennsylvania Avenue (Washington, D.C.)">Pennsylvania Avenue</a>, Washington, D. C.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fiscal_conservatism" title="Fiscal conservatism">Fiscal conservatism</a> is the economic philosophy of prudence in government spending and debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> In his <i><a href="/wiki/Reflections_on_the_Revolution_in_France" title="Reflections on the Revolution in France">Reflections on the Revolution in France</a></i>, Edmund Burke argued that a government does not have the right to run up large debts and then throw the burden on the taxpayer: </p><blockquote><p>[I]t is to the property of the citizen, and not to the demands of the creditor of the state, that the first and original faith of civil society is pledged. The claim of the citizen is prior in time, paramount in title, superior in equity. The fortunes of individuals, whether possessed by acquisition or by descent or in virtue of a participation in the goods of some community, were no part of the creditor's security, expressed or implied...[T]he public, whether represented by a monarch or by a senate, can pledge nothing but the public estate; and it can have no public estate except in what it derives from a just and proportioned imposition upon the citizens at large.</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_conservatism">National conservatism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: National conservatism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/National_conservatism" title="National conservatism">National conservatism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gianfranco_Fini_2004.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="795" decoding="async" height="321" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Gianfranco_Fini_2004.jpg/170px-Gianfranco_Fini_2004.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Gianfranco_Fini_2004.jpg/255px-Gianfranco_Fini_2004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Gianfranco_Fini_2004.jpg/340px-Gianfranco_Fini_2004.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gianfranco_Fini_2004.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gianfranco_Fini" title="Gianfranco Fini">Gianfranco Fini</a>, former President of the Italian <a href="/wiki/Chamber_of_Deputies_(Italy)" title="Chamber of Deputies (Italy)">Chamber of Deputies</a>, in 2004</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/National_conservatism" title="National conservatism">National conservatism</a> is a political term used primarily in Europe to describe a variant of conservatism which concentrates more on national interests than standard conservatism as well as upholding cultural and ethnic identity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> while not being outspokenly <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalist</a> or supporting a <a href="/wiki/Far-right_politics" title="Far-right politics">far-right</a> approach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In Europe, national conservatives are usually <a href="/wiki/Euroscepticism" title="Euroscepticism">eurosceptics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gua_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-gua-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>National conservatism is heavily oriented towards the traditional <a href="/wiki/Family" title="Family">family</a> and social stability as well as in favour of limiting <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a>. As such, national conservatives can be distinguished from economic conservatives, for whom free market economic policies, <a href="/wiki/Deregulation" title="Deregulation">deregulation</a> and fiscal conservatism are the main priorities. Some commentators have identified a growing gap between national and economic conservatism: "[M]ost parties of the Right [today] are run by economic conservatives who, in varying degrees, have marginalized social, cultural, and national conservatives".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> National conservatism is also related to <a href="/wiki/Traditionalist_conservatism" title="Traditionalist conservatism">traditionalist conservatism</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditionalist_conservatism">Traditionalist conservatism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Traditionalist conservatism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_conservatism" title="Traditional conservatism">Traditional conservatism</a></div>
<p>Traditionalist conservatism is a political philosophy emphasizing the need for the principles of <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a> and transcendent moral order, <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">tradition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hierarchy" title="Hierarchy">hierarchy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organic_unity" title="Organic unity">organic unity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agrarianism" title="Agrarianism">agrarianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Classicism" title="Classicism">classicism</a> and <a href="/wiki/High_culture" title="High culture">high culture</a> as well as the intersecting spheres of loyalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Some traditionalists have embraced the labels "<a href="/wiki/Reactionary" title="Reactionary">reactionary</a>" and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counterrevolution" title="Counterrevolution">counterrevolutionary</a>", defying the stigma that has attached to these terms since the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>. Having a hierarchical view of society, many traditionalist conservatives, including a few Americans (notable examples including <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Adams_Cram" title="Ralph Adams Cram">Ralph Adams Cram</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Solange_Hertz" title="Solange Hertz">Solange Hertz</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_S._Lind" title="William S. Lind">William S. Lind</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Charles_A._Coulombe" title="Charles A. Coulombe">Charles A. Coulombe</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>), defend the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy" title="Monarchy">monarchical</a> political structure as the most natural and beneficial social arrangement.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_conservatism">Cultural conservatism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cultural conservatism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_conservatism" title="Cultural conservatism">Cultural conservatism</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_conservative" title="Cultural conservative">Cultural conservatives</a> support the preservation of the heritage of one nation, or of a shared culture that is not defined by national boundaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The shared culture may be as divergent as <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chinese_culture" title="Chinese culture">Chinese culture</a>. In the United States, the term "cultural conservative" may imply a conservative position in the <a href="/wiki/Culture_war" title="Culture war">culture war</a>. Cultural conservatives hold fast to traditional ways of thinking even in the face of monumental change. They believe strongly in traditional values and traditional politics and often have an urgent sense of nationalism.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_conservatism">Social conservatism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Social conservatism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism" title="Social conservatism">Social conservatism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism" title="Social conservatism">Social conservatism</a> is distinct from cultural conservatism, although there are some overlaps. Social conservatives may believe that society is built upon a fragile network of relationships which need to be upheld through duty, traditional values and established institutions;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeywood201769_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeywood201769-37">[37]</a></sup> and that the government has a role in encouraging or enforcing traditional values or behaviours. A social conservative wants to preserve traditional morality and social mores, often by opposing what they consider radical policies or <a href="/wiki/Social_engineering_(political_science)" title="Social engineering (political science)">social engineering</a>. Social change is generally regarded as suspect.
</p><p>Social conservatives today generally favour the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-abortion" title="Anti-abortion">anti-abortion</a> position in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abortion_controversy" title="Abortion controversy">abortion controversy</a> and oppose human <a href="/wiki/Embryonic_stem_cell" title="Embryonic stem cell">embryonic stem cell</a> research (particularly if publicly funded); oppose both <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_enhancement" title="Human enhancement">human enhancement</a> (<a href="/wiki/Transhumanism" title="Transhumanism">transhumanism</a>) while supporting <a href="/wiki/Bioconservatism" title="Bioconservatism">bioconservatism</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> support a traditional definition of marriage as being one man and one woman; view the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a> model as society's foundational unit; oppose expansion of <a href="/wiki/Civil_marriage" title="Civil marriage">civil marriage</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_adoption" title="Child adoption">child adoption</a> to couples in <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_relationship" title="Same-sex relationship">same-sex relationships</a>; promote <a href="/wiki/Public_morality" title="Public morality">public morality</a> and traditional <a href="/wiki/Family_values" title="Family values">family values</a>; oppose <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a>, especially militant atheism, and <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secularism</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MA1_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-MA1-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MA2_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-MA2-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Secularism_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Secularism-41">[41]</a></sup> support the prohibition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prohibition_(drugs)" title="Prohibition (drugs)">drugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prostitution" title="Prostitution">prostitution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euthanasia" title="Euthanasia">euthanasia</a>; and support the <a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship">censorship</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pornography" title="Pornography">pornography</a> and what they consider to be <a href="/wiki/Obscenity" title="Obscenity">obscenity</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indecency" title="Indecency">indecency</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_conservatism">Religious conservatism <span class="anchor" id="Religious_conservatism"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Religious conservatism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Conservative_Christianity" title="Conservative Christianity">Conservative Christianity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Democracy" title="Christian Democracy">Christian Democracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_right" title="Christian right">Christian right</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamism</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_fundamentalism" title="Religious fundamentalism">Religious fundamentalism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marche_pour_la_vie_2012_-_6.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1368" data-file-width="1824" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Marche_pour_la_vie_2012_-_6.jpg/220px-Marche_pour_la_vie_2012_-_6.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Marche_pour_la_vie_2012_-_6.jpg/330px-Marche_pour_la_vie_2012_-_6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Marche_pour_la_vie_2012_-_6.jpg/440px-Marche_pour_la_vie_2012_-_6.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marche_pour_la_vie_2012_-_6.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>2012 <a href="/wiki/March_for_Life_(Paris)" title="March for Life (Paris)">March for Life</a> in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a></div></div></div>
<p>Religious conservatism principally applies the teachings of particular religions to politics: sometimes by merely proclaiming the value of those teachings; at other times, by having those teachings influence laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>In most democracies, political conservatism seeks to uphold traditional family structures and social values. Religious conservatives typically oppose abortion, <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">LGBT</a> behavior (or, in certain cases, identity), drug use,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> and sexual activity outside of marriage. In some cases, conservative values are grounded in religious beliefs, and conservatives seek to increase the role of religion in public life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paternalistic_conservatism">Paternalistic conservatism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Paternalistic conservatism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Paternalistic_conservatism" title="Paternalistic conservatism">Paternalistic conservatism</a></div>
<p>Paternalistic conservatism is a strand in conservatism which reflects the belief that societies exist and develop organically and that members within them have obligations towards each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeywood201334_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeywood201334-45">[45]</a></sup> There is particular emphasis on the <a href="/wiki/Noblesse_oblige" title="Noblesse oblige">paternalistic obligation</a> of those who are <a href="/wiki/Social_privilege" title="Social privilege">privileged</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wealth" title="Wealth">wealthy</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poorer parts of society</a>. Since it is consistent with principles such as <a href="/wiki/Organic_unity" title="Organic unity">organicism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hierarchy" title="Hierarchy">hierarchy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duty" title="Duty">duty</a>, it can be seen as an outgrowth of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_conservatism" title="Traditional conservatism">traditional conservatism</a>. Paternal conservatives support neither the <a href="/wiki/Individual" title="Individual">individual</a> nor the <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a> in principle, but are instead prepared to support either or recommend a balance between the two depending on what is most practical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeywood201280_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeywood201280-46">[46]</a></sup> Paternalistic conservatives historically favor a more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristocratic" title="Aristocratic">aristocratic</a> view (as opposed to the more monarchist traditionalist conservatism) and are ideologically related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_Tories" title="High Tories">High Tories</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In more contemporary times, its proponents stress the importance of a <a href="/wiki/Social_safety_net" title="Social safety net">social safety net</a> to deal with <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a>, support for limited <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Redistribution_of_wealth" title="Redistribution of wealth">redistribution of wealth</a> along with government regulation of markets in the interests of both consumers and producers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patrick_Dunleavy_2000._pp._107–108_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patrick_Dunleavy_2000._pp._107–108-47">[47]</a></sup> Paternalistic conservatism first arose as a distinct ideology in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> under Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a>'s "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One_nation_conservatism" title="One nation conservatism">One Nation</a>" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toryism" title="Toryism">Toryism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patrick_Dunleavy_2000._pp._107–108_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Patrick_Dunleavy_2000._pp._107–108-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> There have been a variety of one nation conservative governments. In the United Kingdom, the Prime Ministers Disraeli, <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Baldwin" title="Stanley Baldwin">Stanley Baldwin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> were or are one nation conservatives.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, during the 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Germany" title="Chancellor of Germany">German Chancellor</a> <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a> adopted policies of state-organized compulsory insurance for workers against sickness, accident, incapacity and old age. Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Leo_von_Caprivi" title="Leo von Caprivi">Leo von Caprivi</a> promoted a conservative agenda called the "New Course".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Progressive_conservatism">Progressive conservatism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Progressive conservatism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the United States, <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> has been the main figure identified with <a href="/wiki/Progressive_conservatism" title="Progressive conservatism">progressive conservatism</a> as a political tradition. Roosevelt stated that he had "always believed that wise progressivism and wise conservatism go hand in hand".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-51">[51]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> administration of President <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a> was a progressive conservative and he described himself as "a believer in progressive conservatism"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-51">[51]</a></sup> and President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> declared himself an advocate of "progressive conservatism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, a variety of conservative governments have been part of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Tory" title="Red Tory">Red Tory</a> tradition, with Canada's former major conservative party being named the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Conservative_Party_of_Canada" title="Progressive Conservative Party of Canada">Progressive Conservative Party of Canada</a> from 1942 to 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hugh_Segal_2011._pp._113–148_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hugh_Segal_2011._pp._113–148-53">[53]</a></sup> In Canada, the Prime Ministers <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Meighen" title="Arthur Meighen">Arthur Meighen</a>, <a href="/wiki/R._B._Bennett" title="R. B. Bennett">R. B. Bennett</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Diefenbaker" title="John Diefenbaker">John Diefenbaker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Clark" title="Joe Clark">Joe Clark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brian_Mulroney" title="Brian Mulroney">Brian Mulroney</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kim_Campbell" title="Kim Campbell">Kim Campbell</a> led Red tory federal governments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hugh_Segal_2011._pp._113–148_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hugh_Segal_2011._pp._113–148-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Authoritarian_conservatism">Authoritarian conservatism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Authoritarian conservatism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Far-right_politics" title="Far-right politics">Far-right politics</a></div>
<p><b>Authoritarian conservatism</b> or <b>reactionary conservatism</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> refers to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autocratic" title="Autocratic">autocratic</a> regimes that center their ideology around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservative_nationalism" title="Conservative nationalism">conservative nationalism</a>, rather than <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_nationalism" title="Ethnic nationalism">ethnic nationalism</a>, though certain racial components such as <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">antisemitism</a> may exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Authoritarian conservative movements show strong devotion towards religion, tradition and culture while also expressing fervent nationalism akin to other far-right nationalist movements. Examples of authoritarian conservative leaders include <a href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_de_Oliveira_Salazar" title="António de Oliveira Salazar">António de Oliveira Salazar</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Engelbert_Dollfuss" title="Engelbert Dollfuss">Engelbert Dollfuss</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Authoritarian conservative movements were prominent in the same era as fascism, with which it sometimes clashed. Although both ideologies shared core values such as nationalism and had common enemies such as <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a>, there was nonetheless a contrast between the traditionalist nature of authoritarian conservatism and the revolutionary, <a href="/wiki/Palingenetic_ultranationalism" title="Palingenetic ultranationalism">palingenetic</a> and populist nature of fascism—thus it was common for authoritarian conservative regimes to suppress rising fascist and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Socialism" title="National Socialism">National Socialist</a> movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The hostility between the two ideologies is highlighted by the struggle for power for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austrian_National_Socialism" title="Austrian National Socialism">National Socialists</a> in Austria, which was marked by the assassination of <a href="/wiki/Engelbert_Dollfuss" title="Engelbert Dollfuss">Engelbert Dollfuss</a>.
</p><p>Sociologist <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Martin_Lipset" title="Seymour Martin Lipset">Seymour Martin Lipset</a> has examined the class basis of right-wing extremist politics in the 1920–1960 era. He reports:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Conservative or rightist extremist movements have arisen at different periods in modern history, ranging from the <a href="/wiki/Mikl%C3%B3s_Horthy" title="Miklós Horthy">Horthyites</a> in Hungary, the <a href="/wiki/Engelbert_Dollfuss" title="Engelbert Dollfuss">Christian Social Party of Dollfuss in Austria</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Der_Stahlhelm,_Bund_der_Frontsoldaten" title="Der Stahlhelm, Bund der Frontsoldaten">Der Stahlhelm</a></i> and other nationalists in pre-Hitler Germany, and <a href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_de_Oliveira_Salazar" title="António de Oliveira Salazar">Salazar in Portugal</a>, to the pre-1966 Gaullist movements and the monarchists in contemporary France and Italy. The right extremists are conservative, not revolutionary. They seek to change political institutions in order to preserve or restore cultural and economic ones, while extremists of the <a href="/wiki/Centrism" title="Centrism">centre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">left</a> seek to use political means for cultural and social revolution. The ideal of the right extremist is not a totalitarian ruler, but a monarch, or a traditionalist who acts like one. Many such movements in Spain, Austria, Hungary, Germany, and Italy-have been explicitly monarchist... The supporters of these movements differ from those of the centrists, tending to be wealthier, and more religious, which is more important in terms of a potential for mass support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_conservative_thought">History of conservative thought</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: History of conservative thought">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3><p>
In Great Britain, the <a href="/wiki/Tory" title="Tory">Tory</a> movement during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Restoration_(England)" title="Restoration (England)">Restoration</a> period (1660–1688) was a precursor to conservatism. Toryism supported a hierarchical society with a monarch who ruled by <a href="/wiki/Divine_right_of_kings" title="Divine right of kings">divine right</a>. However, <a href="/wiki/Tories_(British_political_party)" title="Tories (British political party)">Tories</a> differ from conservatives in that they opposed the idea that sovereignty derived from the people and rejected the authority of parliament and freedom of religion. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Filmer" title="Robert Filmer">Robert Filmer</a>'s <i>Patriarcha: or the Natural Power of Kings</i> (published posthumously in 1680, but written before the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> of 1642–1651) became accepted as the statement of their doctrine. However, the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> of 1688 destroyed this principle to some degree by establishing a constitutional government in England, leading to the hegemony of the Tory-opposed <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whig</a> ideology. Faced with defeat, the Tories reformed their movement. They adopted more conservative positions, such as holding that sovereignty was vested in the three estates of Crown, Lords, and Commons<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> rather than solely in the Crown. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hooker" title="Richard Hooker">Richard Hooker</a> (1554–1600), <a href="/wiki/George_Savile,_1st_Marquess_of_Halifax" title="George Savile, 1st Marquess of Halifax">Marquess of Halifax</a> (1633–1695) and <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> (1711-1776) were proto-conservatives of the period. Halifax promoted pragmatism in government whilst Hume argued against political rationalism and utopianism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EdmundBurke1771.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="672" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/EdmundBurke1771.jpg/170px-EdmundBurke1771.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/EdmundBurke1771.jpg/255px-EdmundBurke1771.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/EdmundBurke1771.jpg/340px-EdmundBurke1771.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EdmundBurke1771.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">Edmund Burke</a> (1729–1797)</div></div></div> <p><a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">Edmund Burke</a> (1729–1797) has been widely regarded as the philosophical founder of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_conservatism" title="Modern conservatism">modern conservatism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Burke served as the private secretary to the <a href="/wiki/Charles_Watson-Wentworth,_2nd_Marquess_of_Rockingham" title="Charles Watson-Wentworth, 2nd Marquess of Rockingham">Marquis of Rockingham</a> and as official pamphleteer to the <a href="/wiki/Rockingham_Whigs" title="Rockingham Whigs">Rockingham branch of the Whig party</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Together with the Tories, they were the conservatives in the late 18th century United Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Burke's views were a mixture of conservatism and republicanism. He supported the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> of 1775–1783 but abhorred the violence of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> (1789–1799). He accepted the conservative ideals of private property and the economics of <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> (1723–1790), but thought that economics should remain subordinate to the conservative social ethic, that capitalism should be subordinate to the medieval social tradition and that the business class should be subordinate to <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> He insisted on standards of honor derived from the medieval aristocratic tradition and saw the aristocracy as the nation's natural leaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> That meant limits on the powers of the Crown, since he found the institutions of Parliament to be better informed than commissions appointed by the executive. He favored an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Established_church" title="Established church">established church</a>, but allowed for a degree of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_toleration" title="Religious toleration">religious toleration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Burke ultimately justified the social order on the basis of tradition: tradition represented the wisdom of the species and he valued community and social harmony over social reforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jmaistre.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="899" data-file-width="683" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Jmaistre.jpg/170px-Jmaistre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Jmaistre.jpg/255px-Jmaistre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Jmaistre.jpg/340px-Jmaistre.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jmaistre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Joseph_de_Maistre" title="Joseph de Maistre">Joseph de Maistre</a> (1753–1821)</div></div></div><p>Another form of conservatism developed in France in parallel to conservatism in Britain. It was influenced by <a href="/wiki/Counter-Enlightenment" title="Counter-Enlightenment">Counter-Enlightenment</a> works by men such as <a href="/wiki/Joseph_de_Maistre" title="Joseph de Maistre">Joseph de Maistre</a> (1753-1821) and <a href="/wiki/Louis_de_Bonald" title="Louis de Bonald">Louis de Bonald</a> (1754-1840). Many continental conservatives do not support <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">separation of church and state</a>, with most supporting state recognition of and cooperation with the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church_in_France" title="Catholic Church in France">Catholic Church</a>, such as had existed in France before the Revolution. Conservatives were also early to embrace nationalism, which was previously associated with liberalism and the Revolution in France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Another early French conservative, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois-Ren%C3%A9_de_Chateaubriand" title="François-René de Chateaubriand">François-René de Chateaubriand</a> (1768-1848), espoused a <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">romantic</a> opposition to modernity, contrasting its emptiness with the 'full heart' of traditional faith and loyalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Elsewhere on the continent, German thinkers <a href="/wiki/Justus_M%C3%B6ser" title="Justus Möser">Justus Möser</a> (1720-1794) and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_von_Gentz" title="Friedrich von Gentz">Friedrich von Gentz</a> (1764-1832) criticized the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen" title="Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen">Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen</a> that came of the Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Opposition was also expressed by <a href="/wiki/August_Wilhelm_Rehberg" title="August Wilhelm Rehberg">August Wilhelm Rehberg</a> (1757-1836), <a href="/wiki/Adam_M%C3%BCller" title="Adam Müller">Adam Müller</a> (1779-1829) and <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel</a> (1771-1830), the latter inspiring both left and right-wing followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both Burke and Maistre were critical of pure democracy in general, though their reasons differed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Maistre was pessimistic about humans being able to follow rules, while Burke was skeptical about humans' innate ability to make rules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> For Maistre, rules had a divine origin, while Burke believed they arose from custom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The lack of custom for Burke, and the lack of divine guidance for Maistre, meant that people would act in terrible ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Both also believed that liberty of the wrong kindled to bewilderment and political breakdown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Their ideas would together flow into a stream of anti-rationalist conservatism, but would still stay separate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Whereas Burke was more open to argumentation and disagreement, Maistre wanted authority and obedience, leading to a more illiberal strain of thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_conservative_parties_and_movements">History of conservative parties and movements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: History of conservative parties and movements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Conservative political parties vary widely from country to country in the goals they wish to achieve. Both conservative and liberal parties tend to favor private ownership of property, in opposition to communist, socialist and green parties, which favor communal ownership or laws requiring social responsibility on the part of property owners. Where conservatives and liberals differ is primarily on social issues. Conservatives tend to reject behavior that does not <a href="/wiki/Conformity" title="Conformity">conform</a> to some <a href="/wiki/Social_norm" title="Social norm">social norm</a>. Modern conservative parties often define themselves by their opposition to liberal or labor parties. The United States usage of the term "conservative" is unique to that country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Italy, which was united by liberals and radicals (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Risorgimento" title="Risorgimento">Risorgimento</a></i>), liberals, not conservatives, emerged as the party of the right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In the Netherlands, conservatives merged into a new Christian democratic party in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> In Austria, Germany, Portugal and Spain, conservatism was transformed into and incorporated into fascism or the <a href="/wiki/Far-right_politics" title="Far-right politics">far-right</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> In 1940, all Japanese parties were merged into a single fascist party. Following the war, Japanese conservatives briefly returned to politics, but were largely purged from public office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Conservative elites have long dominated Latin American nations. Mostly, this has been achieved through control of and support for civil institutions, the church and the armed forces, rather than through party politics. Typically, the church was exempt from taxes and its employees immune from civil prosecution. Where national conservative parties were weak or non-existent, conservatives were more likely to rely on military dictatorship as a preferred form of government. However, in some nations where the elites were able to mobilize popular support for conservative parties, longer periods of political stability were achieved. Chile, Colombia and Venezuela are examples of nations that developed strong conservative parties. Argentina, Brazil, El Salvador and Peru are examples of nations where this did not occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The Conservative Party of Venezuela disappeared following the <a href="/wiki/Federal_War" title="Federal War">Federal Wars</a> of 1858–1863.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Chile's conservative party, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Party_(Chile,_1966%E2%80%931973)" title="National Party (Chile, 1966–1973)">National Party</a>, disbanded in 1973 following a military coup and did not re-emerge as a political force following the subsequent return to democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Louis_Hartz" title="Louis Hartz">Louis Hartz</a> explained conservatism in Quebec and Latin America as a result of their settlement as feudal societies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The American conservative writer <a href="/wiki/Russell_Kirk" title="Russell Kirk">Russell Kirk</a> provided the opinion that conservatism had been brought to the United States and interpreted the American Revolution as a "conservative revolution".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historic_conservatism_in_different_countries">Historic conservatism in different countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Historic conservatism in different countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although political conservatism developed in most countries, most countries did not have conservative parties. Many conservatives parties disappeared as the reasons for their existence disappeared. Below are listed the historic conservative parties that survive today.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Belgium">Belgium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Belgium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Having its roots in the conservative <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Party_(Belgium)" title="Catholic Party (Belgium)">Catholic Party</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christen-Democratisch_en_Vlaams" title="Christen-Democratisch en Vlaams">Christian People's Party</a> retained a conservative edge through the twentieth century, supporting the king in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Question" title="Royal Question">Royal Question</a>, supporting <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a> as the cornerstone of society, defending Christian education, and opposing <a href="/wiki/Euthanasia" title="Euthanasia">euthanasia</a>. The Christian People's Party dominated politics in post-war Belgium. In 1999, the party's support collapsed, and it became the country's fifth-largest party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Currently, the <a href="/wiki/New_Flemish_Alliance" title="New Flemish Alliance">N-VA (nieuw-vlaamse alliantie/New Flemish Alliance)</a> is the largest party in Belgium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_Canada" title="Conservatism in Canada">Conservatism in Canada</a></div>
<p>Canada's conservatives had their roots in the Tory loyalists who left America after the American Revolution. They developed in the socio-economic and political cleavages that existed during the first three decades of the 19th century and had the support of the business, professional and established Church (Anglican) elites in Ontario and to a lesser extent in Quebec. Holding a monopoly over administrative and judicial offices, they were called the "<a href="/wiki/Family_Compact" title="Family Compact">Family Compact</a>" in Ontario and the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chateau_Clique" title="Chateau Clique">Chateau Clique</a>" in Quebec. <a href="/wiki/John_A._Macdonald" title="John A. Macdonald">John A. Macdonald</a>'s successful leadership of the movement to confederate the provinces and his subsequent tenure as prime minister for most of the late 19th century rested on his ability to bring together the English-speaking Protestant oligarchy and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultramontane" title="Ultramontane">ultramontane</a> Catholic hierarchy of Quebec and to keep them united in a conservative coalition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>The conservatives combined <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">pro-market liberalism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toryism" title="Toryism">Toryism</a>. They generally supported an activist government and state intervention in the marketplace and their policies were marked by <i><a href="/wiki/Noblesse_oblige" title="Noblesse oblige">noblesse oblige</a></i>, a paternalistic responsibility of the elites for the less well-off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> From 1942, the party was known as the Progressive Conservatives until 2003, when the national party merged with the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Alliance" title="Canadian Alliance">Canadian Alliance</a> to form the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_of_Canada" title="Conservative Party of Canada">Conservative Party of Canada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>The conservative and <a href="/wiki/Autonomism_in_Quebec" title="Autonomism in Quebec">autonomist</a> <a href="/wiki/Union_Nationale_(Quebec)" title="Union Nationale (Quebec)">Union Nationale</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Duplessis" title="Maurice Duplessis">Maurice Duplessis</a>, governed the province of Quebec in periods from 1936 to 1960 and in a close alliance with the Catholic Church, small rural elites, farmers and business elites. This period, known by liberals as the <a href="/wiki/Grande_Noirceur" title="Grande Noirceur">Great Darkness</a>, ended with the <a href="/wiki/Quiet_Revolution" title="Quiet Revolution">Quiet Revolution</a> and the party went into terminal decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> By the end of the 1960s, the political debate in Quebec centered around the question of independence, opposing the <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social democratic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quebec_sovereignty_movement" title="Quebec sovereignty movement">sovereignist</a> <a href="/wiki/Parti_Qu%C3%A9b%C3%A9cois" title="Parti Québécois">Parti Québécois</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Centrism" title="Centrism">centrist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Federalism_in_Quebec" title="Federalism in Quebec">federalist</a> <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Liberal_Party" title="Quebec Liberal Party">Quebec Liberal Party</a>, therefore marginalizing the conservative movement. Most French Canadian conservatives rallied either the <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Liberal_Party" title="Quebec Liberal Party">Quebec Liberal Party</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Parti_Qu%C3%A9b%C3%A9cois" title="Parti Québécois">Parti Québécois</a>, while some of them still tried to offer an autonomist third-way with what was left of the <a href="/wiki/Union_Nationale_(Quebec)" title="Union Nationale (Quebec)">Union Nationale</a> or the more <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populists</a> <a href="/wiki/Ralliement_cr%C3%A9ditiste_du_Qu%C3%A9bec" title="Ralliement créditiste du Québec">Ralliement créditiste du Québec</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parti_national_populaire" title="Parti national populaire">Parti national populaire</a>, but by the <a href="/wiki/1981_Quebec_general_election" title="1981 Quebec general election">1981 provincial election</a> politically organized conservatism had been obliterated in Quebec. It slowly started to revive at the <a href="/wiki/1994_Quebec_general_election" title="1994 Quebec general election">1994 provincial election</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Action_d%C3%A9mocratique_du_Qu%C3%A9bec" title="Action démocratique du Québec">Action démocratique du Québec</a>, who served as <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_opposition" title="Parliamentary opposition">Official opposition</a> in the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Quebec" title="National Assembly of Quebec">National Assembly</a> from 2007 to 2008, before its merger with <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Legault" title="François Legault">François Legault's</a> <a href="/wiki/Coalition_Avenir_Qu%C3%A9bec" title="Coalition Avenir Québec">Coalition Avenir Québec</a> in 2012, that took power in 2018.
</p><p>The modern <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_of_Canada" title="Conservative Party of Canada">Conservative Party of Canada</a> has rebranded conservatism and under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Harper" title="Stephen Harper">Stephen Harper</a>, the Conservative Party added more conservative policies.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Colombia">Colombia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Colombia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_Colombia" title="Conservatism in Colombia">Conservatism in Colombia</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Colombian_Conservative_Party" title="Colombian Conservative Party">Colombian Conservative Party</a>, founded in 1849, traces its origins to opponents of General <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Paula_Santander" title="Francisco de Paula Santander">Francisco de Paula Santander</a>'s 1833–1837 administration. While the term "liberal" had been used to describe all political forces in Colombia, the conservatives began describing themselves as "conservative liberals" and their opponents as "red liberals". From the 1860s until the present, the party has supported strong central government; supported the Catholic Church, especially its role as protector of the sanctity of the family; and opposed separation of church and state. Its policies include the legal equality of all men, the citizen's right to own property and opposition to dictatorship. It has usually been Colombia's second largest party, with the <a href="/wiki/Colombian_Liberal_Party" title="Colombian Liberal Party">Colombian Liberal Party</a> being the largest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Denmark">Denmark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Denmark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Founded in 1915, the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_People%27s_Party_(Denmark)" title="Conservative People's Party (Denmark)">Conservative People's Party of Denmark</a> was the successor of <i><a href="/wiki/H%C3%B8jre" title="Højre">Højre</a></i> (literally "<a href="/wiki/Right-wing_politics" title="Right-wing politics">Right"</a>). Another Danish conservative party was the <a href="/wiki/Free_Conservatives" title="Free Conservatives">Free Conservatives</a> who were active between 1902 and 1920. The Conservative People's Party led the government coalition from 1982 to 1993. The party had previously been member of various governments from 1916 to 1917, 1940 to 1945, 1950 to 1953 and 1968 to 1971. The party was a junior partner in coalition with the <a href="/wiki/Venstre_(Denmark)" title="Venstre (Denmark)">Liberals</a> from 2001 to 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> The party is preceded by 11 years by the <a href="/wiki/Young_Conservatives_(Denmark)" title="Young Conservatives (Denmark)">Young Conservatives (KU)</a>, today the youth movement of the party. The party suffered a major defeat in the parliamentary elections of September 2011 in which the party lost more than half of its seat and also lost governmental power. A liberal cultural policy dominated during the post-war period.
