However, by the 1990s, disagreements regarding immigrants from entirely different cultures ignited a conservative backlash.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> In 2015 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nye_Borgerlige" title="Nye Borgerlige">Nye Borgerlige</a> (The New Right) was founded promoting themselves as “true conservatives” and claiming that the Conservative People's Party had left its 'original values' behind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Since January 2021 though the Danish Opinion polls has often shown the Conservative People's Party to be the second most popular political party in Denmark among the Danish electorates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The conservative parties in Denmark have always considered the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Denmark" title="Monarchy of Denmark">monarchy</a> as a central institution in Denmark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Finland">Finland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Finland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The conservative party in Finland is the <a href="/wiki/National_Coalition_Party" title="National Coalition Party">National Coalition Party</a> (in Finnish <i>Kansallinen Kokoomus</i>, <i>Kok</i>). The party was founded in 1918 when several monarchist parties united. Although in the past the party was right-wing, today it is a moderate liberal conservative party. While the party advocates <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economic liberalism</a>, it is committed to the <a href="/wiki/Social_market_economy" title="Social market economy">social market economy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_europeanpeoplesparty_-_EPP_Summit_October_2010_(105)-(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1454" data-file-width="979" decoding="async" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Flickr_-_europeanpeoplesparty_-_EPP_Summit_October_2010_%28105%29-%28cropped%29.jpg/200px-Flickr_-_europeanpeoplesparty_-_EPP_Summit_October_2010_%28105%29-%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Flickr_-_europeanpeoplesparty_-_EPP_Summit_October_2010_%28105%29-%28cropped%29.jpg/300px-Flickr_-_europeanpeoplesparty_-_EPP_Summit_October_2010_%28105%29-%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Flickr_-_europeanpeoplesparty_-_EPP_Summit_October_2010_%28105%29-%28cropped%29.jpg/400px-Flickr_-_europeanpeoplesparty_-_EPP_Summit_October_2010_%28105%29-%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_europeanpeoplesparty_-_EPP_Summit_October_2010_(105)-(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a>, <a href="/wiki/President_of_France" title="President of France">President of France</a> (2007–2012)</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gaullism" title="Gaullism">Gaullism</a></div>
<p>Conservatism in France focused on the rejection of the secularism of the French Revolution, support for the role of the Catholic Church and the restoration of the monarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> The monarchist cause was on the verge of victory in the 1870s, but then collapsed because the proposed king, <a href="/wiki/Henri,_Count_of_Chambord" title="Henri, Count of Chambord">Henri, Count of Chambord</a>, refused to fly the tri-colored flag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Religious tensions heightened in the 1890–1910 era, but moderated after the spirit of unity in fighting the First World War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> An extreme form of conservatism characterized the <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">Vichy regime</a> of 1940–1944 with heightened antisemitism, opposition to individualism, emphasis on family life and national direction of the economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the Second World War, conservatives in France supported Gaullist groups and have been nationalistic and emphasized tradition, order and the regeneration of France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Gaullists held divergent views on social issues. The number of conservative groups, their lack of stability and their tendency to be identified with local issues defy simple categorization. Conservatism has been the major political force in France since the Second World War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Unusually, post-war French conservatism was formed around the personality of a leader, <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a>; and did not draw on traditional French conservatism, but on the <a href="/wiki/Bonapartism" title="Bonapartism">Bonapartism</a> tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Gaullism in France continues under <a href="/wiki/The_Republicans_(France)" title="The Republicans (France)">The Republicans</a> (formerly <a href="/wiki/Union_for_a_Popular_Movement" title="Union for a Popular Movement">Union for a Popular Movement</a>), which was previously led by <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a>, a conservative figure in France. (see <a href="/wiki/Sinistrisme" title="Sinistrisme">Sinistrisme</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The word "conservative" itself is a term of abuse to many people in France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greece">Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The main inter-war conservative party was called the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Party_(Greece)" title="People's Party (Greece)">People's Party</a> (PP), which supported <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_monarchy" title="Greek monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> and opposed the <a href="/wiki/Second_Hellenic_Republic" title="Second Hellenic Republic">republican</a> <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(Greece)" title="Liberal Party (Greece)">Liberal Party</a>. Both it and the Liberal party were suppressed by the authoritarian, arch-conservative and royalist <a href="/wiki/4th_of_August_Regime" title="4th of August Regime">4th of August Regime</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ioannis_Metaxas" title="Ioannis Metaxas">Ioannis Metaxas</a> in 1936–1941. The PP was able to re-group after the Second World War as part of a United Nationalist Front which achieved power campaigning on a simple anticommunist, ultranationalist platform during the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Civil_War" title="Greek Civil War">Greek Civil War</a> (1946–1949). However, the vote received by the PP declined during the so-called "Centrist Interlude" in 1950–1952. In 1952, Marshal <a href="/wiki/Alexandros_Papagos" title="Alexandros Papagos">Alexandros Papagos</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Rally" title="Greek Rally">Greek Rally</a> as an umbrella for the right-wing forces. The Greek Rally came to power in 1952 and remained the leading party in Greece until 1963—after Papagos' death in 1955 reformed as the <a href="/wiki/National_Radical_Union" title="National Radical Union">National Radical Union</a> under <a href="/wiki/Konstantinos_Karamanlis" title="Konstantinos Karamanlis">Konstantinos Karamanlis</a>. Right-wing governments backed by the palace and the army overthrew the <a href="/wiki/Centre_Union" title="Centre Union">Centre Union</a> government in 1965 and governed the country until the establishment of the far-right <a href="/wiki/Greek_junta" title="Greek junta">Greek junta</a> (1967–1974). After the <a href="/wiki/Metapolitefsi" title="Metapolitefsi">regime's collapse</a> in August 1974, Karamanlis returned from exile to lead the government and founded the <a href="/wiki/New_Democracy_(Greece)" title="New Democracy (Greece)">New Democracy</a> party. The new conservative party had four objectives: to confront <a href="/wiki/Turkish_invasion_of_Cyprus" title="Turkish invasion of Cyprus">Turkish expansionism in Cyprus</a>, to reestablish and solidify democratic rule, to give the country a strong government and to make a powerful moderate party a force in Greek politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Independent_Greeks" title="Independent Greeks">Independent Greeks</a>, a newly formed political party in <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, has also supported conservatism, particularly <a href="/wiki/National_conservatism" title="National conservatism">national</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_conservatism" title="Religious conservatism">religious conservatism</a>. The Founding Declaration of the Independent Greeks strongly emphasises in the preservation of the Greek state and its <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_people" title="Greek people">Greek people</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iceland">Iceland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Iceland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Founded in 1924 as the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(Iceland)" title="Conservative Party (Iceland)">Conservative Party</a>, Iceland's <a href="/wiki/Independence_Party_(Iceland)" title="Independence Party (Iceland)">Independence Party</a> adopted its current name in 1929 after the merger with the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(Iceland,_historical)" title="Liberal Party (Iceland, historical)">Liberal Party</a>. From the beginning, they have been the largest vote-winning party, averaging around 40%. They combined liberalism and conservatism, supported nationalization of infrastructure and opposed class conflict. While mostly in opposition during the 1930s, they embraced <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economic liberalism</a>, but accepted the welfare state after the war and participated in governments supportive of state intervention and protectionism. Unlike other Scandanivian conservative (and liberal) parties, it has always had a large working-class following.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> After the financial crisis in 2008, the party has sunk to a lower support level around 20–25%.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Luxembourg">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Luxembourg's major conservative party, the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Social_People%27s_Party" title="Christian Social People's Party">Christian Social People's Party</a> (CSV or PCS), was formed as the Party of the Right in 1914 and adopted its present name in 1945. It was consistently the largest political party in Luxembourg, and dominated politics throughout the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Norway">Norway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Norway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(Norway)" title="Conservative Party (Norway)">Conservative Party of Norway</a> (Norwegian: Høyre, literally "<a href="/wiki/Right-wing_politics" title="Right-wing politics">right"</a>) was formed by the old upper class of state officials and wealthy merchants to fight the populist democracy of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(Norway)" title="Liberal Party (Norway)">Liberal Party</a>, but lost power in 1884, when parliamentarian government was first practised. It formed its first government under parliamentarism in 1889 and continued to alternate in power with the Liberals until the 1930s, when Labour became the dominant political party. It has elements both of paternalism, stressing the responsibilities of the state, and of economic liberalism. It first returned to power in the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A5re_Willoch#Premiership" title="Kåre Willoch">Kåre Willoch's premiership</a> in the 1980s, much emphasis was laid on liberalizing the credit and housing market, and abolishing the <a href="/wiki/NRK" title="NRK">NRK</a> TV and radio monopoly, while supporting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_and_order_(politics)" title="Law and order (politics)">law and order</a> in criminal justice and traditional norms in education<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden">Sweden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Sweden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Sweden's conservative party, the <a href="/wiki/Moderate_Party" title="Moderate Party">Moderate Party</a>, was formed in 1904, two years after the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Liberals_(Sweden)#History" title="Liberals (Sweden)">Liberal Party</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> The party emphasizes tax reductions, deregulation of private enterprise and privatization of schools, hospitals, and kindergartens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Switzerland">Switzerland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Switzerland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are a number of conservative parties in Switzerland's parliament, the Federal Assembly. These include the largest, the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_People%27s_Party" title="Swiss People's Party">Swiss People's Party</a> (SVP),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hans-Georg_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hans-Georg-130">[130]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Democratic_People%27s_Party_of_Switzerland" title="Christian Democratic People's Party of Switzerland">Christian Democratic People's Party</a> (CVP)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Juravich_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Juravich-131">[131]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Democratic_Party_of_Switzerland" title="Conservative Democratic Party of Switzerland">Conservative Democratic Party of Switzerland</a> (BDP),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwok_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwok-132">[132]</a></sup> which is a splinter of the SVP created in the aftermath to the election of Eveline Widmer-Schlumpf as Federal Council.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwok_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schwok-132">[132]</a></sup> The right-wing parties have a majority in the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Assembly_(Switzerland)" title="Federal Assembly (Switzerland)">Federal Assembly</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Swiss_People%27s_Party" title="Swiss People's Party">Swiss People's Party</a> (SVP or UDC) was formed from the 1971 merger of the <a href="/wiki/Party_of_Farmers,_Traders_and_Independents" title="Party of Farmers, Traders and Independents">Party of Farmers, Traders and Citizens</a>, formed in 1917 and the smaller Swiss Democratic Party, formed in 1942. The SVP emphasized agricultural policy and was strong among farmers in German-speaking Protestant areas. As Switzerland considered closer relations with the European Union in the 1990s, the SVP adopted a more militant protectionist and isolationist stance. This stance has allowed it to expand into German-speaking Catholic mountainous areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Anti-Defamation_League" title="Anti-Defamation League">Anti-Defamation League</a>, a non-Swiss lobby group based in the United States has accused them of manipulating issues such as immigration, Swiss neutrality and welfare benefits, awakening antisemitism and racism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> has called the SVP "extreme right", although some scholars dispute this classification. For instance, Hans-Georg Betz describes it as "populist radical right".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> The SVP is the largest party since 2003.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ukraine">Ukraine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Ukraine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Authoritarian <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_State" title="Ukrainian State">Ukrainian State</a> headed by <a href="/wiki/Pavlo_Skoropadskyi" title="Pavlo Skoropadskyi">Pavlo Skoropadskyi</a> represented the conservative movement. The 1918 <a href="/wiki/Hetman_of_Ukraine" title="Hetman of Ukraine">Hetman</a> government, which appealed to the tradition of the 17th–18th century <a href="/wiki/Cossack_Hetmanate" title="Cossack Hetmanate">Cossack Hetman state</a>, represented the conservative strand in Ukraine's struggle for independence. It had the support of the proprietary classes and of conservative and moderate political groups. <a href="/wiki/Vyacheslav_Lypynsky" title="Vyacheslav Lypynsky">Vyacheslav Lypynsky</a> was an main ideologue of Ukrainian conservatism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Conservatism in the United Kingdom">Conservatism in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<p>According to historian James Sack, English conservatives celebrate <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">Edmund Burke</a> who was Irish, as their intellectual father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Burke was affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whig Party</a> which eventually became the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a>, but the modern <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> is generally thought to derive from the <a href="/wiki/Tories_(British_political_party)" title="Tories (British political party)">Tory party</a> and the MPs of the modern conservative party are still frequently referred to as Tories.
</p><p>Shortly after Burke's death in 1797, conservatism revived as a mainstream political force as the Whigs suffered a series of internal divisions. This new generation of conservatives derived their politics not from Burke, but from his predecessor, the <a href="/wiki/Henry_St_John,_1st_Viscount_Bolingbroke" title="Henry St John, 1st Viscount Bolingbroke">Viscount Bolingbroke</a> (1678–1751), who was a Jacobite and traditional Tory, lacking Burke's sympathies for Whiggish policies such as <a href="/wiki/Catholic_emancipation" title="Catholic emancipation">Catholic emancipation</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American independence</a> (famously attacked by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a> in "Taxation No Tyranny"). In the first half of the 19th century, many newspapers, magazines, and journals promoted loyalist or right-wing attitudes in religion, politics and international affairs. Burke was seldom mentioned, but <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Younger" title="William Pitt the Younger">William Pitt the Younger</a> (1759–1806) became a conspicuous hero. The most prominent journals included <i>The Quarterly Review</i>, founded in 1809 as a counterweight to the Whigs' <i>Edinburgh Review</i> and the even more conservative <i>Blackwood's Edinburgh Magazine</i>. Sack finds that the <i>Quarterly Review</i> promoted a balanced Canningite toryism as it was neutral on Catholic emancipation and only mildly critical of Nonconformist Dissent; it opposed slavery and supported the current poor laws; and it was "aggressively imperialist". The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-church" title="High-church">high-church</a> clergy of the Church of England read the <i><a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Churchman%27s_Magazine" title="Orthodox Churchman's Magazine">Orthodox Churchman's Magazine</a></i> which was equally hostile to Jewish, Catholic, <a href="/wiki/Jacobin" title="Jacobin">Jacobin</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methodist" title="Methodist">Methodist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a> spokesmen. Anchoring the ultra Tories, <i>Blackwood's Edinburgh Magazine</i> stood firmly against Catholic emancipation and favoured slavery, cheap money, mercantilism, the <a href="/wiki/Navigation_Acts" title="Navigation Acts">Navigation Acts</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Alliance" title="Holy Alliance">Holy Alliance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>Conservatism evolved after 1820, embracing free trade in 1846 and a commitment to democracy, especially under Disraeli. The effect was to significantly strengthen conservatism as a grassroots political force. Conservatism no longer was the philosophical defense of the landed aristocracy, but had been refreshed into redefining its commitment to the ideals of order, both secular and religious, expanding imperialism, strengthened monarchy and a more generous vision of the welfare state as opposed to the punitive vision of the Whigs and liberals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> As early as 1835, Disraeli attacked the Whigs and utilitarians as slavishly devoted to an industrial oligarchy, while he described his fellow Tories as the only "really democratic party of England" and devoted to the interests of the whole people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Nevertheless, inside the party there was a tension between the growing numbers of wealthy businessmen on the one side and the aristocracy and rural gentry on the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The aristocracy gained strength as businessmen discovered they could use their wealth to buy a peerage and a country estate.
</p><p>Although conservatives opposed attempts to allow greater representation of the middle class in parliament, they conceded that <a href="/wiki/Electoral_reform" title="Electoral reform">electoral reform</a> could not be reversed and promised to support further reforms so long as they did not erode the institutions of church and state. These new principles were presented in the <a href="/wiki/Tamworth_Manifesto" title="Tamworth Manifesto">Tamworth Manifesto</a> of 1834, which historians regard as the basic statement of the beliefs of the new Conservative Party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Robert_Peel_Portrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1104" data-file-width="808" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Robert_Peel_Portrait.jpg/170px-Robert_Peel_Portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Robert_Peel_Portrait.jpg/255px-Robert_Peel_Portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Robert_Peel_Portrait.jpg/340px-Robert_Peel_Portrait.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Robert_Peel_Portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Robert Peel</a> (1788–1850)</div></div></div>
<p>Some conservatives lamented the passing of a pastoral world where the ethos of <i><a href="/wiki/Noblesse_oblige" title="Noblesse oblige">noblesse oblige</a></i> had promoted respect from the lower classes. They saw the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglican_Church" title="Anglican Church">Anglican Church</a> and the aristocracy as balances against commercial wealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> They worked toward legislation for improved working conditions and urban housing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> This viewpoint would later be called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tory_democracy" title="Tory democracy">Tory democracy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> However, since Burke, there has always been tension between traditional aristocratic conservatism and the wealthy business class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1834, Tory <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Robert Peel</a> issued the <a href="/wiki/Tamworth_Manifesto" title="Tamworth Manifesto">Tamworth Manifesto</a> in which he pledged to endorse moderate political reform. This marked the beginning of the transformation of British conservatism from <a href="/wiki/High_Tory" title="High Tory">High Tory</a> reactionism towards a more modern form based on "conservation". The party became known as the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> as a result, a name it has retained to this day. However, Peel would also be the root of a split in the party between the traditional Tories (led by the <a href="/wiki/Edward_Smith-Stanley,_14th_Earl_of_Derby" title="Edward Smith-Stanley, 14th Earl of Derby">Earl of Derby</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a>) and the "Peelites" (led first by Peel himself, then by the <a href="/wiki/George_Hamilton-Gordon,_4th_Earl_of_Aberdeen" title="George Hamilton-Gordon, 4th Earl of Aberdeen">Earl of Aberdeen</a>). The split occurred in 1846 over the issue of <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a>, which Peel supported, versus <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionism</a>, supported by Derby. The majority of the party sided with Derby whilst about a third split away, eventually merging with the <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whigs</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radicalism_(politics)" title="Radicalism (politics)">radicals</a> to form the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a>. Despite the split, the mainstream Conservative Party accepted the doctrine of free trade in 1852.
</p><p>In the second half of the 19th century, the Liberal Party faced political schisms, especially over <a href="/wiki/History_of_Ireland" title="History of Ireland">Irish</a> <a href="/wiki/Irish_Parliamentary_Party" title="Irish Parliamentary Party">Home Rule</a>. Leader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Gladstone" title="William Gladstone">William Gladstone</a> (himself a former Peelite) sought to give Ireland a degree of autonomy, a move that elements in both the left and right-wings of his party opposed. These split off to become the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Unionist_Party" title="Liberal Unionist Party">Liberal Unionists</a> (led by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a>), forming a coalition with the Conservatives before merging with them in 1912. The Liberal Unionist influence dragged the Conservative Party towards the left as Conservative governments passing a number of progressive reforms at the turn of the 20th century. By the late 19th century, the traditional business supporters of the Liberal Party had joined the Conservatives, making them the party of <a href="/wiki/Business" title="Business">business</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commerce" title="Commerce">commerce</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>After a period of Liberal dominance before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a>, the Conservatives gradually became more influential in government, regaining full control of the cabinet in 1922. In the inter-war period, conservatism was the major ideology in Britain<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> as the Liberal Party vied with the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> for control of the left. After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, the first Labour government (1945–1951) under <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a> embarked on a program of nationalization of industry and the promotion of social welfare. The Conservatives generally accepted those policies until the 1980s.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_stock_portrait_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1099" data-file-width="790" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Margaret_Thatcher_stock_portrait_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Margaret_Thatcher_stock_portrait_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Margaret_Thatcher_stock_portrait_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Margaret_Thatcher_stock_portrait_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Margaret_Thatcher_stock_portrait_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Margaret_Thatcher_stock_portrait_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_stock_portrait_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> (1925–2013), under whose leadership the Conservative Party has shifted their economic policies to the right as well as <a href="/wiki/Thatcherism" title="Thatcherism">Thatcherism</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the 1980s, the Conservative government of <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a>, guided by <a href="/wiki/Neoliberalism" title="Neoliberalism">neoliberal</a> economics, reversed many of Labour's programmes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> The Conservative Party also adopt <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_euroscepticism" title="Soft euroscepticism">soft eurosceptic</a> politics, and oppose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Europe" title="Federal Europe">Federal Europe</a>. Other conservative political parties, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Independence_Party" title="United Kingdom Independence Party">United Kingdom Independence Party</a> (UKIP, founded in 1993), <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionist_Party" title="Ulster Unionist Party">Ulster Unionist Party</a> (UUP) and the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Unionist_Party" title="Democratic Unionist Party">Democratic Unionist Party</a> (DUP, founded in 1971), began to appear, although they have yet to make any significant impact at Westminster (as of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the DUP comprises the largest political party in the ruling coalition in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">Northern Ireland Assembly</a>), and from 2017 to 2019 the DUP provided support for the Conservative <a href="/wiki/Second_May_ministry" title="Second May ministry">minority government</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_conservatism_in_different_countries">Modern conservatism in different countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Modern conservatism in different countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many sources refer to any political parties on the right of the political spectrum as conservative despite having no connection with historical conservatism. In most cases, these parties do not use the term conservative in their name or self-identify as conservative. Below is a partial list of such political parties.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_Australia" title="Conservatism in Australia">Conservatism in Australia</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_of_Australia" title="Liberal Party of Australia">Liberal Party of Australia</a> adheres to the principles of <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism" title="Social conservatism">social conservatism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_conservatism" title="Liberal conservatism">liberal conservatism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> It is liberal in the sense of economics. Other conservative parties are the <a href="/wiki/National_Party_of_Australia" title="National Party of Australia">National Party of Australia</a>, a sister party of the Liberals, <a href="/wiki/Family_First_Party" title="Family First Party">Family First Party</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Labour_Party_(Australia)" title="Democratic Labour Party (Australia)">Democratic Labor Party</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shooters,_Fishers_and_Farmers_Party" title="Shooters, Fishers and Farmers Party">Shooters, Fishers and Farmers Party</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australian_Conservatives" title="Australian Conservatives">Australian Conservatives</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Katter%27s_Australian_Party" title="Katter's Australian Party">Katter's Australian Party</a>.
</p><p>The second largest party in the country is the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Labor_Party" title="Australian Labor Party">Australian Labor Party</a> and its dominant faction is <a href="/wiki/Labor_Right" title="Labor Right">Labor Right</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socially_conservative" title="Socially conservative">socially conservative</a> element. Australia undertook significant economic reform under the Labor Party in the mid-1980s. Consequently, issues like protectionism, welfare reform, privatization and deregulation are no longer debated in the political space as they are in Europe or North America. Moser and Catley explain: "In America, 'liberal' means left-of-center, and it is a pejorative term when used by conservatives in adversarial political debate. In Australia, of course, the conservatives are in the Liberal Party."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Jupp writes that "[the] decline in English influences on Australian reformism and radicalism, and appropriation of the symbols of Empire by conservatives continued under the Liberal Party leadership of Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Menzies" title="Robert Menzies">Robert Menzies</a>, which lasted until 1966".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Brazil">Brazil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Brazil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_Brazil" title="Conservatism in Brazil">Conservatism in Brazil</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jair_Bolsonaro_2020_(cropped_2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1782" data-file-width="1366" decoding="async" height="287" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Jair_Bolsonaro_2020_%28cropped_2%29.jpg/220px-Jair_Bolsonaro_2020_%28cropped_2%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Jair_Bolsonaro_2020_%28cropped_2%29.jpg/330px-Jair_Bolsonaro_2020_%28cropped_2%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Jair_Bolsonaro_2020_%28cropped_2%29.jpg/440px-Jair_Bolsonaro_2020_%28cropped_2%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jair_Bolsonaro_2020_(cropped_2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jair_Bolsonaro" title="Jair Bolsonaro">Jair Bolsonaro</a>, the incumbent President of Brazil, known for his conservative stances</div></div></div>
<p>Conservatism in Brazil originates from the cultural and historical tradition of Brazil, whose cultural roots are <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Luso</a>-<a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Brazilian conservatism from the 20th century on includes names such as <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A1rio_Ferreira_dos_Santos" title="Mário Ferreira dos Santos">Mário Ferreira dos Santos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vicente_Ferreira_da_Silva" title="Vicente Ferreira da Silva">Vicente Ferreira da Silva</a> in philosophy; <a href="/wiki/Gerardo_Melo_Mour%C3%A3o" title="Gerardo Melo Mourão">Gerardo Melo Mourão</a> and <a href="/wiki/Otto_Maria_Carpeaux" title="Otto Maria Carpeaux">Otto Maria Carpeaux</a> in literature; <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Tolentino" title="Bruno Tolentino">Bruno Tolentino</a> in poetry; <a href="/wiki/Olavo_de_Carvalho" title="Olavo de Carvalho">Olavo de Carvalho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paulo_Francis" title="Paulo Francis">Paulo Francis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lu%C3%ADs_Ernesto_Lacombe" title="Luís Ernesto Lacombe">Luís Ernesto Lacombe</a> in journalism; <a href="/wiki/Manuel_de_Oliveira_Lima" title="Manuel de Oliveira Lima">Manuel de Oliveira Lima</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jo%C3%A3o_Camilo_de_Oliveira_Torres" title="João Camilo de Oliveira Torres">João Camilo de Oliveira Torres</a> in historiography; <a href="/wiki/Sobral_Pinto" title="Sobral Pinto">Sobral Pinto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miguel_Reale" title="Miguel Reale">Miguel Reale</a> in law; <a href="/wiki/Gustavo_Cor%C3%A7%C3%A3o" title="Gustavo Corção">Gustavo Corção</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plinio_Corr%C3%AAa_de_Oliveira" title="Plinio Corrêa de Oliveira">Plinio Corrêa de Oliveira</a>, <a href="/wiki/Father_L%C3%A9o" title="Father Léo">Father Léo</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Father_Paulo_Ricardo" title="Father Paulo Ricardo">Father Paulo Ricardo</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roberto_de_Oliveira_Campos" title="Roberto de Oliveira Campos">Roberto Campos</a> and <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A1rio_Henrique_Simonsen" title="Mário Henrique Simonsen">Mario Henrique Simonsen</a> in economics; <a href="/wiki/Joaquim_Nabuco" title="Joaquim Nabuco">Joaquim Nabuco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Lacerda" title="Carlos Lacerda">Carlos Lacerda</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> and incumbent president <a href="/wiki/Jair_Bolsonaro" title="Jair Bolsonaro">Jair Bolsonaro</a> in the political arena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Brazil_Union" title="Brazil Union">Brazil Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Progressistas" title="Progressistas">Progressistas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republicans_(Brazil)" title="Republicans (Brazil)">Republicans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(Brazil,_2006)" title="Liberal Party (Brazil, 2006)">Liberal Party</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Labour_Renewal_Party" title="Brazilian Labour Renewal Party">Brazilian Labour Renewal Party</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patriota" title="Patriota">Patriota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Labour_Party_(current)" title="Brazilian Labour Party (current)">Brazilian Labour Party</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_Christian_Party_(Brazil)" title="Social Christian Party (Brazil)">Social Christian Party</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Party_of_the_Brazilian_Woman" title="Party of the Brazilian Woman">Brasil 35</a> are the conservative parties in Brazil.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_Germany" title="Conservatism in Germany">Conservatism in Germany</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Angela_Merkel_2016.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1297" data-file-width="1056" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Angela_Merkel_2016.jpg/200px-Angela_Merkel_2016.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Angela_Merkel_2016.jpg/300px-Angela_Merkel_2016.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Angela_Merkel_2016.jpg/400px-Angela_Merkel_2016.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Angela_Merkel_2016.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Angela_Merkel" title="Angela Merkel">Angela Merkel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Germany" title="Chancellor of Germany">Chancellor of Germany</a> from 2005 to 2021</div></div></div><p>
Conservatism developed alongside nationalism in Germany, culminating in Germany's victory over France in the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">Franco-Prussian War</a>, the creation of the unified <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> in 1871 and the simultaneous rise of <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a> on the European political stage. Bismarck's "balance of power" model maintained peace in Europe for decades at the end of the 19th century. His "revolutionary conservatism" was a conservative state-building strategy designed to make ordinary Germans—not just the Junker elite—more loyal to state and emperor, he created the modern welfare state in Germany in the 1880s. According to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kees_van_Kersbergen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kees van Kersbergen (page does not exist)">Kees van Kersbergen</a> and Barbara Vis, his strategy was: <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[G]ranting social rights to enhance the integration of a hierarchical society, to forge a bond between workers and the state so as to strengthen the latter, to maintain traditional relations of authority between social and status groups, and to provide a countervailing power against the modernist forces of liberalism and socialism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Bismarck also enacted universal male suffrage in the new German Empire in 1871.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> He became a great hero to German conservatives, who erected many monuments to his memory after he left office in 1890.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the rise of <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a> in 1933, <a href="/wiki/Agrarianism" title="Agrarianism">agrarian movements</a> faded and was supplanted by a more command-based economy and forced social integration. Though <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> succeeded in garnering the support of many German industrialists, prominent traditionalists openly and secretly opposed his policies of euthanasia, genocide and attacks on organized religion, including <a href="/wiki/Claus_von_Stauffenberg" title="Claus von Stauffenberg">Claus von Stauffenberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_Bonhoeffer" title="Dietrich Bonhoeffer">Dietrich Bonhoeffer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henning_von_Tresckow" title="Henning von Tresckow">Henning von Tresckow</a>, Bishop <a href="/wiki/Clemens_August_Graf_von_Galen" title="Clemens August Graf von Galen">Clemens August Graf von Galen</a> and the monarchist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Goerdeler" title="Carl Friedrich Goerdeler">Carl Friedrich Goerdeler</a>.
</p><p>
More recently, the work of conservative <a href="/wiki/Christian_Democratic_Union_of_Germany" title="Christian Democratic Union of Germany">Christian Democratic Union</a> leader and Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Helmut_Kohl" title="Helmut Kohl">Helmut Kohl</a> helped bring about <a href="/wiki/German_reunification" title="German reunification">German reunification</a>, along with the closer <a href="/wiki/European_integration" title="European integration">European integration</a> in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">Maastricht Treaty</a>.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Angela_Merkel_(9307204896).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3280" data-file-width="4928" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Angela_Merkel_%289307204896%29.jpg/200px-Angela_Merkel_%289307204896%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Angela_Merkel_%289307204896%29.jpg/300px-Angela_Merkel_%289307204896%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Angela_Merkel_%289307204896%29.jpg/400px-Angela_Merkel_%289307204896%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Angela_Merkel_(9307204896).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/European_People%27s_Party" title="European People's Party">European People's Party</a> (EPP) Congress in <a href="/wiki/Bucharest" title="Bucharest">Bucharest</a> in 2012, whose three conservative leaders in the EPP included <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Spain" title="Prime Minister of Spain">Prime Minister of Spain</a> <a href="/wiki/Mariano_Rajoy" title="Mariano Rajoy">Mariano Rajoy</a> (2011–2018), <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Germany" title="Chancellor of Germany">Chancellor of Germany</a> <a href="/wiki/Angela_Merkel" title="Angela Merkel">Angela Merkel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Hungary" title="Prime Minister of Hungary">Prime Minister of Hungary</a> <a href="/wiki/Viktor_Orb%C3%A1n" title="Viktor Orbán">Viktor Orbán</a></div></div></div>
<p>Today, German conservatism is often associated with politicians such as Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Angela_Merkel" title="Angela Merkel">Angela Merkel</a>, whose tenure has been marked by attempts to save the common European currency (<a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">Euro</a>) from demise. The German conservatives are divided under Merkel due to the refugee crisis in Germany and many conservatives in the <a href="/wiki/CDU/CSU" title="CDU/CSU">CDU/CSU</a> oppose the refugee and migrant policies developed under Merkel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_India" title="Conservatism in India">Conservatism in India</a></div>
<p>In India, the <a href="/wiki/Bharatiya_Janata_Party" title="Bharatiya Janata Party">Bharatiya Janata Party</a> (BJP), led by <a href="/wiki/Narendra_Modi" title="Narendra Modi">Narendra Modi</a>, represent conservative politics. The BJP is the largest right-wing conservative party in the world. It promotes cultural nationalism, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu_Nationalism" title="Hindu Nationalism">Hindu Nationalism</a>, an aggressive foreign policy against Pakistan and a conservative social and fiscal policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy">Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>By 1945 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Fascism" title="Italian Fascism">extreme right fascist</a> movement of <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> was discredited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> 
After World War II, in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> the conservative parties were dominated by the centrist <a href="/wiki/Christian_Democracy_(Italy)" title="Christian Democracy (Italy)">Christian Democracy</a> (DC) party. With its landslide victory over the left in 1948, the center (including progressive and conservative factions) was in power and was, says Denis Mack Smith, "moderately conservative, reasonably tolerant of everything which did not touch religion or property, but above all Catholic and sometimes clerical."  It dominated politics until the DC party's dissolution in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1994, the media tycoon and entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/Silvio_Berlusconi" title="Silvio Berlusconi">Silvio Berlusconi</a> founded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberal_conservative" title="Liberal conservative">liberal conservative</a> party <a href="/wiki/Forza_Italia" title="Forza Italia">Forza Italia</a> (FI). Berlusconi won three elections in <a href="/wiki/1994_Italian_general_election" title="1994 Italian general election">1994</a>, <a href="/wiki/2001_Italian_general_election" title="2001 Italian general election">2001</a> and <a href="/wiki/2008_Italian_general_election" title="2008 Italian general election">2008</a>, governing the country for almost ten years as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Italy" title="Prime Minister of Italy">Prime Minister</a>. Forza Italia formed a coalition with right-wing regional party <a href="/wiki/Lega_Nord" title="Lega Nord">Lega Nord</a> while in government.  Besides FI, now the conservative ideas are mainly expressed by the <a href="/wiki/New_Centre-Right" title="New Centre-Right">New Centre-Right</a> party led by <a href="/wiki/Angelino_Alfano" title="Angelino Alfano">Angelino Alfano</a>, Berlusconi formed a new party, which is a rebirth of <a href="/wiki/Forza_Italia_(2013)" title="Forza Italia (2013)">Forza Italia</a>, thus founding a new conservative movement. Alfano served as <a href="/wiki/Italian_Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs" title="Italian Minister of Foreign Affairs">Minister of Foreign Affairs</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/2018_Italian_general_election" title="2018 Italian general election">2018 election</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lega_Nord" title="Lega Nord">Lega Nord</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Five_Star_Movement" title="Five Star Movement">Five Star Movement</a> formed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right-wing_populist" title="Right-wing populist">right-wing populist</a> government, which later failed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Under <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a>, the dominant leader since 1999, Russia has promoted explicitly conservative policies in social, cultural and political matters, both at home and abroad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> Putin has attacked globalism and economic liberalism. Russian conservatism is unique in some respects as it supports <a href="/wiki/Economic_interventionism" title="Economic interventionism">Economic intervention</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Mixed_economy" title="Mixed economy">mixed economy</a>, with a strong <a href="/wiki/Russian_nationalism" title="Russian nationalism">nationalist sentiment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism" title="Social conservatism">social conservatism</a> with its views being largely <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_populism" title="Right-wing populism">populist</a>. Russian conservatism as a result opposes <a href="/wiki/Right-libertarianism" title="Right-libertarianism">libertarian</a> ideals such as the aforementioned concept of <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economic liberalism</a> found in other conservative movements around the world. Putin has as a result promoted new <a href="/wiki/Think_tank" title="Think tank">think tanks</a> that bring together like-minded intellectuals and writers. For example, the Izborsky Club, founded in 2012 by Aleksandr Prokhanov, stresses Russian nationalism, the restoration of Russia's historical greatness and systematic opposition to liberal ideas and policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vladislav_Surkov" title="Vladislav Surkov">Vladislav Surkov</a>, a senior government official, has been one of the key ideologists during Putin's presidency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup>
</p><p>In cultural and social affairs, Putin has collaborated closely with the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a>. Mark Woods provides specific examples of how the Church under <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_Kirill_of_Moscow" title="Patriarch Kirill of Moscow">Patriarch Kirill of Moscow</a> has backed the expansion of Russian power into Crimea and eastern Ukraine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> More broadly, <i>The New York Times</i> reports in September 2016 how that Church's policy prescriptions support the Kremlin's appeal to social conservatives:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"A fervent foe of homosexuality and any attempt to put individual rights above those of family, community, or nation, the Russian Orthodox Church helps project Russia as the natural ally of all those who pine for a more secure, illiberal world free from the tradition-crushing rush of globalization, multiculturalism, and women's and gay rights."</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Andrew Higgins (<a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a>: In Expanding Russian Influence, Faith Combines With Firepower)</cite></div></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Korea">South Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: South Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_South_Korea" title="Conservatism in South Korea">Conservatism in South Korea</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>'s major conservative party, the <a href="/wiki/People_Power_Party_(South_Korea)" title="People Power Party (South Korea)">People Power Party (South Korea)</a>, has changed its form throughout its history. First it was the Democratic-Liberal Party(민주자유당, Minju Ja-yudang) and its first head was <a href="/wiki/Roh_Tae-woo" title="Roh Tae-woo">Roh Tae-woo</a> who was the first President of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sixth_Republic_of_South_Korea" title="Sixth Republic of South Korea">Sixth Republic of South Korea</a>. Democratic-Liberal Party was founded by the merging of <a href="/wiki/Roh_Tae-woo" title="Roh Tae-woo">Roh Tae-woo</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Justice_Party" title="Democratic Justice Party">Democratic Justice Party</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kim_Young_Sam" title="Kim Young Sam">Kim Young Sam</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Reunification_Democratic_Party" title="Reunification Democratic Party">Reunification Democratic Party</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kim_Jong-pil" title="Kim Jong-pil">Kim Jong-pil</a>'s New Democratic Republican Party. And again through election its second leader, <a href="/wiki/Kim_Young-sam" title="Kim Young-sam">Kim Young-sam</a>, became the fourteenth President of Korea. When the conservative party was beaten by the opposition party in the general election, it changed its form again to follow the party members' demand for reforms. It became the New Korean Party, but it changed again one year later since the President Kim Young-sam was blamed by the citizen for the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (March 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It changed its name to Grand National Party (GNP). Since the late <a href="/wiki/Kim_Dae-jung" title="Kim Dae-jung">Kim Dae-jung</a> assumed the presidency in 1998, GNP had been the opposition party until <a href="/wiki/Lee_Myung-bak" title="Lee Myung-bak">Lee Myung-bak</a> won the presidential election of <a href="/wiki/2007_South_Korean_presidential_election" title="2007 South Korean presidential election">2007</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Singapore">Singapore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Singapore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Lee_Kuan_Yew" title="Lee Kuan Yew">Lee Kuan Yew</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>'s only conservative party is the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Action_Party" title="People's Action Party">People's Action Party</a> (PAP). It is currently in <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Singapore" title="Government of Singapore">government</a> and has been in government since independence in 1965. It has promoted conservative values in the form of Asian democracy and values or 'shared values'. The main party on the left of the political spectrum in Singapore is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Workers%27_Party_of_Singapore" title="Workers' Party of Singapore">Workers' Party</a> (WP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">Conservatism in the United States</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:President_Reagan_speaking_in_Minneapolis_1982.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2337" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/President_Reagan_speaking_in_Minneapolis_1982.jpg/220px-President_Reagan_speaking_in_Minneapolis_1982.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/President_Reagan_speaking_in_Minneapolis_1982.jpg/330px-President_Reagan_speaking_in_Minneapolis_1982.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/President_Reagan_speaking_in_Minneapolis_1982.jpg/440px-President_Reagan_speaking_in_Minneapolis_1982.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:President_Reagan_speaking_in_Minneapolis_1982.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> (1981–1989), whose <a href="/wiki/Reagan_Doctrine" title="Reagan Doctrine">Reagan Doctrine</a> has reshaped the Republican Party</div></div></div>
<p>The meaning of conservatism in the United States has little in common with the way the word is used elsewhere. As Ribuffo (2011) notes, "what Americans now call conservatism much of the world calls liberalism or neoliberalism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> American conservatism is a broad system of political beliefs in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> that is characterized by respect for American traditions, support for <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Christian_ethics" title="Judeo-Christian ethics">Judeo-Christian</a> values, <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economic liberalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-communism</a>, and a defense of <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a>. <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">Liberty</a> within the bounds of conformity to conservatism is a core value, with a particular emphasis on strengthening the <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free market</a>, limiting the size and scope of government and opposition to high taxes and government or labor union encroachment on the entrepreneur.
</p><p>The 1830s <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="History of the Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> became divided between <a href="/wiki/Southern_Democrats" title="Southern Democrats">Southern Democrats</a>, who supported slavery, secession, and later segregation, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Democrats" title="Northern Democrats">Northern Democrats</a>, who tended to support the abolition of slavery, union, and equality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> Many Democrats were conservative in the sense that they wanted things to be like they were in the past, especially as far as race was concerned. They generally favored poorer farmers and urban workers, and were hostile to banks and industrialization and high tariffs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>The post-Civil War <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="History of the Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a> elected the first Blacks to serve in both local and national political office. The Southern Democrats united with pro-segregation Northern Republicans to form the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservative_Coalition" title="Conservative Coalition">Conservative Coalition</a>, which successfully put an end to Blacks being elected to national political office until 1967, when <a href="/wiki/Edward_Brooke" title="Edward Brooke">Edward Brooke</a> was elected Senator from Massachusetts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>In late 19th century, the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> split into two factions; the more conservative Eastern business faction (led by <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a>) favored gold, while the South and West (led by <a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">William Jennings Bryan</a>) wanted more silver in order to raise prices for their crops.  In 1892, Cleveland won the election on a conservative platform, which supported maintaining the gold standard, reducing tariffs, and taking a laisse-faire approach to government intervention. A severe nationwide <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1893" title="Panic of 1893">depression</a> ruined his plans.  Many of his supporters in 1896 supported the <a href="/wiki/National_Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="National Democratic Party (United States)">Gold Democrats</a> when liberal <a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">William Jennings Bryan</a> won the nomination and campaigned for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bimetalism" title="Bimetalism">bimetalism</a>, money backed by both gold and silver. The conservative wing nominated <a href="/wiki/Alton_B._Parker" title="Alton B. Parker">Alton B. Parker</a> in 1904, but he got very few votes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the 1920s, conservatism in the United States has been chiefly associated with the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a>. During the era of <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation_in_the_United_States" title="Racial segregation in the United States">segregation</a>, many <a href="/wiki/Southern_Democrats" title="Southern Democrats">Southern Democrats</a> were conservatives and they played a key role in the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_coalition" title="Conservative coalition">conservative coalition</a> that largely controlled domestic policy in Congress from 1937 to 1963.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Democrat" title="Conservative Democrat">conservative Democrats</a> continued to have influence in the US politics until 1994's <a href="/wiki/Republican_Revolution" title="Republican Revolution">Republican Revolution</a>, when the American South shifted from solid Democrat to solid Republican, while maintaining its conservative values.
</p><p>The major conservative party in the United States today is the Republican Party, also known as the GOP (Grand Old Party). Modern American conservatives consider <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individual liberty</a>, as long as it conforms to conservative values, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small_government" title="Small government">small government</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deregulation" title="Deregulation">deregulation</a> of the government, <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economic liberalism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a>, as the fundamental trait of democracy, which contrasts with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_Liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Modern Liberalism in the United States">modern American liberals</a>, who generally place a greater value on <a href="/wiki/Social_equality" title="Social equality">social equality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> Other major priorities within American conservatism include support for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_family" title="Traditional family">traditional family</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_and_order_(politics)" title="Law and order (politics)">law and order</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_to_bear_arms" title="Right to bear arms">right to bear arms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_values" title="Christian values">Christian values</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-communism</a> and a defense of "<a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Western_civilization" title="Western civilization">Western civilization</a> from the challenges of modernist culture and totalitarian governments".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GregorySchneider_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-GregorySchneider-185">[185]</a></sup> Economic conservatives and libertarians favor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small_government" title="Small government">small government</a>, low taxes, limited regulation and free enterprise. Some social conservatives see traditional social values threatened by secularism, so they support <a href="/wiki/School_prayer" title="School prayer">school prayer</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opposition_to_the_legalization_of_abortion" title="Opposition to the legalization of abortion">oppose abortion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">homosexuality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Social_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Social-186">[186]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neoconservatives" title="Neoconservatives">Neoconservatives</a> want to expand American ideals throughout the world and show a strong support for Israel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleoconservatives" title="Paleoconservatives">Paleoconservatives</a>, in opposition to multiculturalism, press for restrictions on immigration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paleoconservative2_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paleoconservative2-188">[188]</a></sup> Most US conservatives prefer Republicans over Democrats and most factions favor a strong foreign policy and a strong military. The conservative movement of the 1950s attempted to bring together these divergent strands, stressing the need for unity to prevent the spread of "godless communism", which Reagan later labeled an "<a href="/wiki/Evil_Empire_speech" title="Evil Empire speech">evil empire</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reagan_administration" title="Reagan administration">Reagan administration</a>, conservatives also supported the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Reagan_Doctrine" title="Reagan Doctrine">Reagan Doctrine</a>" under which the US as part of a Cold War strategy provided military and other support to guerrilla insurgencies that were fighting governments identified as socialist or communist. The Reagan administration also adopted <a href="/wiki/Neoliberalism" title="Neoliberalism">neoliberalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reaganomics" title="Reaganomics">Reaganomics</a> (pejoratively referred to as <a href="/wiki/Trickle-down_economics" title="Trickle-down economics">trickle-down economics</a>), resulting in the 1980s economic growth and trillion-dollar deficits.
</p><p>Other modern conservative positions include opposition to <a href="/wiki/Big_government" title="Big government">big government</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opposition_to_environmentalism" title="Opposition to environmentalism">opposition to environmentalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> On average, American conservatives desire tougher foreign policies than liberals do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">Economic liberalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deregulation" title="Deregulation">deregulation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism" title="Social conservatism">social conservatism</a> are major principles of the Republican Party.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tea_Party_movement" title="Tea Party movement">Tea Party movement</a>, founded in 2009, had proven a large outlet for populist American conservative ideas. Their stated goals included rigorous adherence to the US constitution, lower taxes, and opposition to a growing role for the federal government in health care. Electorally, it was considered a key force in Republicans reclaiming control of the US House of Representatives in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychology">Psychology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Conservatism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Psychology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Biology_and_political_orientation" title="Biology and political orientation">Biology and political orientation</a></div>
<p>Following the Second World War, psychologists conducted research into the different motives and tendencies that account for ideological differences between left and right. The early studies focused on conservatives, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Theodor_W._Adorno" title="Theodor W. Adorno">Theodor W. Adorno</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Authoritarian_Personality" title="The Authoritarian Personality">The Authoritarian Personality</a></i> (1950) based on the <a href="/wiki/F-scale_(personality_test)" title="F-scale (personality test)">F-scale</a> personality test.
